B009POCG2C|4.0|Whereas MONEYBALL makes a case for the cold hard stats of sabermetrics, THE TROUBLE WITH THE CURVE celebrates the gut instinct and the long years of experience of the baseball scout. In the realm of America's national pastime, old Gus Lobel (Clint Eastwood) roams, a dinosaur in the era of the tech-savvy armchair pundit. Gus is a veteran baseball scout for the Atlanta Braves. In the twilight of his career, his eyes are steadily failing him. It's not fully incapacitated him yet, but he's steady tripping over stuff now. His contract is up in three months. He gets wind that management is set to put him out to pasture. Gus, that stubborn, snarling mule, is just ornery enough to prove them wrong.<br /><br />Mickey (Amy Adams) is Gus's estranged 33-year-old daughter, an ambitious attorney on the cusp of being made partner. She's named after her dad's favorite baseball player. When an old family friend approaches her to relate her father's faltering vision, she's guilted into coming along Gus's latest scouting foray, never mind that Gus is fiercely against her tagging along. That tender father-daughter intimacy thing just ain't his bag.<br /><br />I could've sworn GRAN TORINO was the last bow, and that Eastwood was forever done with the acting side of the industry. But acting's an itch, isn't it? You can say that THE TROUBLE WITH THE CURVE is a bit obvious, and there's no arguing from me. You can say it's too leisurely paced, but that's what you get with character studies. The film works hard to get into the skins of its characters, is diligent with examining the two leads' foibles and quirks and a slew of neuroses that runs bone deep. It peels back layers. There are tiny details and pieces of memory that unfold gradually, the blend and sum of which explain away that monumental rift between Gus and Mickey. Gus is such a defensive, self-reliant old cuss. It literally pains him to have to depend on someone. You never question his daughter's committment to set things right. After all, this is Amy Adams playing the role, and Amy Adams exudes sunshine and light. The question always was will Gus learn to bend and meet her halfway. He's never had before.<br /><br />I'm still not sold on Justin Timberlake as an actor. In each film I'd seen him in before, he seemed to be always \\"on.\\" That feeling doesn't evaporate with his performance here, but he's likable as the once promising pitcher ('til he wrecked his arm) and now fledgling scout for the Red Sox. He strikes an easy familiarity with Eastwood and is off-the-cuff charming with Adams. Timberlake presents the film's secondary and tertiary plotlines. Not only is he Mickey's potential love interest, he and Gus are vying for the same high school power hitter. More instrumental is that he provides the bridge between Gus and Mickey. Who would've thunk it that JT could pull off a part that's funny and diplomatic and wise? But his clogging is horrible.<br /><br />THE TROUBLE WITH THE CURVE works too hard for the perfect conclusion, except by that time I am so invested in the characters and their interactions that damn if I didn't lap it all up, all the convenient but very satisfying endings (Take that, Matthew Lillard!). The narrative won't ambush you with crazy plot twists. The conflicts are ones that play out a lot in typical familial relationship dramas. But Eastwood and Adams' nuanced performances glue you to where you'd parked your butt. I enjoyed THE TROUBLE WITH THE CURVE, but for Eastwood, GRAN TORINO is the better film to go out on. This is a good thing. There's hope that he won't retire just yet from in front of the camera.ter all, this is Amy Adams playing the role, and Amy Adams exudes sunshine and light. B00C8985DU|5.0|There are certain moments that make a rock musician an icon. Paul Mccartney has been blessed with a career with plenty of those moments. A true icon in every sense and not only a member of the royalty of rock but of an even higher status of a mythical god-like status as well.<br /><br />1976 was a major year for Paul Mccartney. Already a dinosaur because of the huge legacy of the Beatles was such a high mark to live up to. Yet, it was like Beatlemania all over again, with sell out concerts, high record sales, and great reviews. One would ask how could someone be as so lucky as to conquer the entire world again--AMERICA as well. Only Peter Frampton comes to mind during this time frame. Wanting to refrain from playing Beatles hits on the early Wings tours, he got a bit nostalgic and decided to pull out all the stops with Wings Over America. But with Wings Over America, Mccartney made icons of his new band too.<br /><br />Mccartney performs with sheer confidence unparalleled by any other artist, and his voice is at it's finest from his wails to his laid back ballad voice as he seemlessly moves from bass to piano to guitar throughout the entire set. B00BTHWFBK|5.0|Others may break down each track of 13. I'll attempt to break down a far bigger picture of what this remarkable album represents.<br /><br />While it appears that the positive reactions to this album will certainly trounce the negative, it has been somewhat astonishing to read some of the criticism surrounding Black Sabbath's 13. Truth be told, despite dipping into the well that brought them fame and fortune, this is a very progressive album and it requires an attentive listener to fully comprehend everything that it is about. Many of these songs are harmonically diverse with complex arrangements, tempo shifts, and utterly fascinating developments with twists and turns that somehow never cross into the \\"pretentious.\\" Instead, the process strikes as organic and most of these tracks attain a very impressive and natural \\"flow\\" to the ideas at hand. True Sabbath fans will recognize this approach all too well and find the greatest joys in hearing a band with this level of musicianship back in action. Casual fans or the uninitiated may need quite a few listens to understand all that is contained within these compositions.<br /><br />How many times have you heard comments that the opening track sounds like the title track of their debut album all those years ago? And yet, that only tells a minor part of the story. \\"The End Of The Beginning\\" is epic Sabbath and a sound that only Sabbath can stretch toward and attain. It opens more like \\"Under The Sun,\\" then finds its way into the \\"Black Sabbath\\" comparisons when the riff softens to that familiar feel, but it then drifts into a riff that would have been comfortable on Master Of Reality until somehow finding Sabotage and finally landing in Technical Ecstasy territory. Naysayers will cite these sections as having been pulled from their 70s heyday, but in reality these ideas have never been juxtaposed in this manner. And somehow, some way ... it all has a certain \\"freshness\\" about it.<br /><br />\\"Zeitgeist\\" is an absolutely breathtaking revisit to the band's \\"spacey,\\" \\"jazzy\\" side. While some will argue that this territory was already explored on \\"Planet Caravan,\\" I would submit that the band is simply taking ownership of yet another sub-genre that they in fact created. I've lost count of how many they have been credited with. Perhaps we can now call this sub-genre the \\"space jazz-metal ballad?\\" In other words, by using a specific approach which they came up with in the first place, they are able to revisit while at the same time pushing the idea forward. Pay attention to the vocal melodies and the way the song takes shape. This isn't \\"Planet Caravan\\" or \\"Solitude.\\" This is the band that *created* \\"Planet Caravan\\" and \\"Solitude.\\" Of course the similarities are there, because nobody sounds like this and they invented it!<br /><br />And that's the thing about Sabbath. The diversity which they were able to find while retaining their trademark \\"heavy\\" sound was often remarkable. Somehow, they were a band that brought innovation to nearly everything they attempted. They were essentially peerless in this respect. Ballads? Sabbath's never sounded like anybody else's. Instrumental, acoustic guitar work? Ok, you tell me about the influences on \\"Don't Start (Too Late).\\" Medieval campfire music? Maybe Robert Fripp? In fact, take any of Sabbath's acoustic work, it sure wasn't \\"West Coast\\" folk music or anything wedged between tracks on other \\"hard rock\\" albums. Nope ... instead, no matter what they did, at the core of Sabbath's 70s run was a constant theme of innovation. Some critics didn't get it. Some rock fans didn't get it. But Sabbath knew their \\"brand.\\" And those who mattered to Sabbath \\"got it.\\" And those fans were almost never disappointed. Some would argue that they had a few missteps, but you find me a guitar player who can even transcribe the solos to songs like \\"You Won't Change Me\\" or \\"Air Dance\\" and I bet I'll find you a guy who doesn't necessarily buy that even at their supposed \\"worst\\" these guys were anything less than ridiculously talented freaks from some other planet of rock music.<br /><br />That same spirit of innovation and belief in the \\"Black Sabbath brand\\" is evident here. Oh sure, it's watered down because of digital recording and horrible, compressed mastering (the CD, that is ... the vinyl sounds far better and you should own it instantly -- it has twice the dynamic range of the CD!), but inside these songs are enough different rides to please even the most difficult to impress \\"old school\\" Sabbath fan. And I won't lie, the fact that these guys worked this hard on this material (don't let their humility in interviews fool you) in their mid 60s is an accomplishment that I suspect is not met with a great many peer. And you're right that because of that I *root* for this album. I also root for this album because Sabbath is the greatest living proof that some of the most devoted rock music fans can't be fooled. Armed with enough nonsense from enough writers who hadn't the faintest clue of the level of musical brilliance that Sabbath possessed or the lyrical insights which they attempted to exchange, the \\"mainstream\\" press couldn't keep these guys down. Fans knew better. Black Sabbath was a \\"people's band\\" and their continued success is a triumph of their ever growing fan base having an intimate understanding of their musical genius.<br /><br />Ward is sadly missed here. If anybody tries to argue that he isn't, then his rock and roll membership card should be subject to immediate revocation. If Bonham played like a horse; galloping through songs with power and driving his band forward like a freight train, Ward was a snake. He slithered through minefields of tempo changes and dynamic shifts with the intensity of a Cobra. He swung with riffs like a Rattlesnake but other times restrained himself like a Python waiting to attack its prey. And oh, let's not forget that he could also pounce upon grooves with the white-hot intensity of molten lava easing its way down a mountainside. He was a human metronome with a jazz sensibility. Arguably the rarest of combinations amongst rock drummers. He helped shape Sabbath into one of the most distinctive sounding acts of the 70s or any other era. But at this stage, I think we must talk about those who came to this party. Mr. Ward's reasons for declining his invitation are perhaps not fully understood, but are surely not without merit. Still, we have reached the point where the music and the concept of Black Sabbath at current must be judged on its own merits, and that means we must acknowledge the reality that the band now exists without Bill Ward.<br /><br />Osbourne's vocal melodies on 13 should remind of just what an important component he was to the Sabbath sound. Does that mean there is a \\"Spiral Architect\\" here? No. Will \\"The Writ\\" be challenged on its throne atop any reasoned list of \\"heavy metal\\" epics? I suspect not. But this album has vocal work that sits on the Moon. It isn't even of this Earth. It's \\"buzzed out,\\" high pitched, wailing ear candy that will sooth your tired brain in this world of American Idolists and trumped up Steve Wonder rip-offs. Osbourne's voice will still stand out a mile when it's heard a hundred years from now. And here, it doesn't matter that he isn't at his absolute technical peak any longer. As a comparison, think of Billie Holiday in some of her most trying, drug-addled performances as the years rolled on. She still packed more spirit in those pipes than a hundred of the best younger and more technical jazz singers combined. It was a combination of ability and a unique, raw force of nature that propelled her voice into your heart. Ozzy sings his heart out for you on this album. Let your ears enjoy it and forget for a moment that he \\"can't sing the highest parts\\" of your favorite mid 70s Sabs track. This is about what he can do. And what he can do still amounts to glorious sounds that others can't attain. It's in the blood ofhis tracks of life.<br /><br />Tony Iommi still plays with an intensity only he can conjure up from the darkest realms of space. A combination of phenomenal, self-taught techniques and gallons upon gallons of soul. Take just the opening chords of \\"The End Of The Beginning\\" as the perfect example. You could ask a thousand \\"brilliant\\" guitarists to play those chords. None would achieve that spine-tingling intensity he does. None would find those perfect places to accentuate that uniquely innovative vibrato he places on full chords. None would sound like him. His leads on this album are extraordinary. They are often improvised and retain an \\"off the cuff\\" feel that he was able to extract on albums like 'Technical Ecstasy' and 'Never Say Die!' Whatever those albums \\"lacked\\" in the ridiculously amazing arsenal of 70s Sabbath, they featured some of the most remarkable guitar work of his generation. Few of his peers landed there. While perhaps his work at this late stage can't *quite* approach his work at that point, he's on fire with passion on this album and he is taking names. Tony Iommi, folks. Tony Iommi. May his work on this record evoke a few tears from your eyes. The man is a machine with a heart.<br /><br />Geezer Butler's bass work reminds us once again why it is possible to find fills and runs that will fit into every groove while never losing it. He's busy here, but as always -- and as is so important to all the members of Sabbath -- his work is disciplined and musical. And his lyrics offer observations of the world in which we now find ourselves. Some won't \\"get\\" them. Some will think they mean things they don't. Others will argue that he can't come up with stuff like he could at his peak on albums like Sabbath Bloody Sabbath or Sabotage. I think he has delivered phenomenal commentaries on modern life and finds valid questions about the belief systems inherent in different walks of life. Religion, politics, computerized society ... The \\"Master Of Reality\\" strikes again.<br /><br />13 is a five star album not because it's Sabbath's best work, but because Sabbath's best work rates as six stars on five star scales. That's the standard which they set. Let this album take you on a journey far away from what has happened to rock music and enjoy every last minute of it. This is a remarkable album from a remarkable team.

B00BTHWFBK|5.0|You will read a lot of reviews out there comparing this work to the original Sabbath albums from 1970 to 1978. These reviews base their opinions on what Sabbath was and it's legacy. The problem with this is that it's 35 years later and a lot of water has gone under that bridge.<br /><br />Comparing this album to those works or to any work of any artist is a natural thing but where it creates a problem is that none of the three members of this band live in a vacuum. In fact, they all have had very public and active lives since 1978 and \\"13\\" is much a reflection of these lives as it is the original works.<br /><br />\\"13\\" is less derivative of anything they have done and more of an homage to the work they created. Clearly, Rick Rubin asked these men to be inspired by the original works but demanded an original creation. What \\"13\\" represents is a culmination of three musical talents looking back and aspiring to make something new yet retaining the character of the old. In this, they succeed beyond question.<br /><br />\\"13\\" definitely feels like old Sabbath from the opening riffs of the \\"End of the Beginning\\" to church bells tolling in the final moments. Each song contains the trademark sounds of Sabbath in its highest and most epic moments. The outstanding guitar work of Tony Iommi, the articulate lyrics and bass of Geezer Butler and the trademark voice of Ozzy Osbourne all combine exactly as they should to make another epic Black Sabbath album.<br /><br />Still, it is a refreshing original record filled with wonderful moments. \\"Damaged Soul\\" and its harmonica laced, bluesy rock or \\"Age of Reason\\" with its monster riffs are evidence that these legends still got game. While other aged artists continue to deliver derivative mediocre, featureless albums, Sabbath has produced something that fits in their catalog but still sounds as fresh as any of the latest releases of a top selling rock artist.<br /><br />Many attribute the invention of Heavy Metal to Black Sabbath and tothis end, this album is solid proof that they are it's inventors and best practitioners. It's darkened, doom laden power permeates every song and delivers metal in its purest form. This album begs to be played loud; annoying the neighbors; shaking the house and scaring the cat.<br /><br />Think of this like a cake your mom used to make. You can put all the ingredients together and bake it yourself but it never turns out the same. Something is missing. Other incarnations of Sabbath and solo projects were great. However, here the original Black Sabbath returns and puts all the same ingredients in and bakes us another delicious cake just like we remember. It might not be exactly the same, but it's damned good just the same.<br /><br />Any fan of the band is going to have a nostalgic turn listening to \\"13.\\" Younger listeners will hear the echoes of the original and might be inspired to go back to the legendary works. In fact, the only people who aren't going to like this album are those expecting it to rival the originals or expecting it to sound like something Sabbath isn't. To that, I say \\"Let them eat cake.\\"

By the 1990s the Stones ended up just repeating the same basic formula with diminishing returns. Goats Head Soup was one of their last attempts to do something original. B00BSO2U1Y|2.0|Gave the new Bartoli Norma a listen to yesterday. It certainly has<br />some points of interest regarding the instruments and edition but<br />there are real problems concerning the actual performance. The two big<br />problems I have are the conducting and the sound. The conducting is<br />constantly in fits and starts with sudden allegros that seem to have<br />no dramatic purpose. What's worse is that this approach makes music in<br />the opera which needs special handling e.g. the march announcing<br />Norma's arrival in Act One which can sound very Italian village<br />\\"banda\\" even more trivial and banal. The end of the Adalgisa-Pollione<br />duet at the end of Act One also sounds ridiculously fast. Needless to<br />say the big double climax at the end of the opera goes for little. In<br />my experience only Serafin and one or two others pace it so it has the<br />overwhelming effect it should have. The other problem is the sound -<br />perhaps in an attempt to make the voices sound larger than they are<br />the voices are way too close. The men don't seem too affected but the<br />sounds of both women suffer from explosive consonants and hissing \\"S's\\".<br />Of the vocal performances, Sumi Jo is easily the best and one of the<br />best Adalgisas I have ever heard. For once, the singer actually sounds<br />like a young girl rather than an old truck driver and the phrasing is<br />beautiful. Her dramatic instincts are spot on as well.<br />Bartoli sings the more meditative and gentle phrases well, so we get a<br />nice picture of the softer side of Norma. But when it comes to warrior<br />Norma or the more declamatory phrases, she has neither the weight of<br />voice, breadth of phrasing, or dramatic instincts to carry it off.<br />When she tries it, the line breaks up and gets breathy and petulant<br />sounding. The entire vocal intro to Casta Diva (Sediozose voce!!)<br />suffers from this and she just doesn't the gravitas and strength to<br />pull off the end.<br />All I can say about the bass and tenor is that one wouldn't mind<br />hearing them on a pleasant evening in a smaller Italian city or as<br />second line singers in a big one, but the singing is hardly worth<br />enshrining on recordings. The Black Ice has some roughness, and could be polished a little further, but it's not that distracting.<br /><br />The Harry Bosch figure is quite original. He had sad childhood, and he had dark war experiences, which gives an additional layer to his character. He is the type who does what is right even if he has to go against administration procedures or direct orders from his superiors. It makes him bigger than life. He also have good instincts.<br /><br />The Black Ice is a classic \\"cop chase the trail and get the bad guy\\" kind of book. Bosch does a lot of investigating and thinking, and there is less fight than in a fast paced action story. There is the \\"final battle\\", though. 0525425772|5.0|John Grisham is best known for his fictional adult books about lawyers. But he has strayed away from that market on more than one occasion with a few other books for adults, as well as his Theodore Boone series for kids, of which this is the fourth.<br /><br />A group of people are pushing for a highway relocation that will move the freeway away from going right through the middle of Strattenberg, where Theo lives, and reroute it around the west side of town. This will ease traffic congestion downtown, make the route shorter for truckers to get their goods from one end of town to the other and provide jobs for hundreds of people. The politicians, big business owners and truckers are all for the new construction.<br /><br />On the surface, it sounds like a good idea. But of course, there are problems with such a grand scheme. For starters, the price tag is 200 million dollars. That's a lot of money. Part of the money will come from federal and state coffers, but a big part of it will be picked up by taxpayers in Strattenberg. That wouldn't be so bad except the city has already had to make huge budget cuts --- such as laying off city workers and teachers --- just to make ends meet. Also, the new highway will go right through a very rural, scenic area. This part of town is home to farms and campgrounds. And the new highway will run within a few hundred yards of a nearby elementary school, dangerously close to the playground there, as well as through part of a relatively new soccer complex on the outskirts of town.<br /><br />Of course the highway project is big news. It's being talked about on local radio and TV stations, and there have been numerous articles about it in the newspaper. Theo is aware of what's going on, but at first he isn't really concerned about the new traffic project. He doesn't really see how it will concern him.<br /><br />But when he finds out that a farm --- one that has been in his friend's family for generations --- will be totally flattened, that the town's water supply will be threatened, and that the children on the school playground will be bombarded with noise and air pollution, he starts to think that maybe the project isn't such a good idea after all.<br /><br />After his dog is attacked by some over zealous surveyors hired by the state, who trespassed on his friend's land, Theo decides to join a group of concerned citizens in the town who are against the highway project. He becomes an activist. He goes up against the big guys in an effort to shut the project down.<br /><br />As always, Grisham's books are entertaining as well as informative. Readers of this book will learn about the legal concept of eminent domain as well as what it's like to battle big interest groups.<br /><br />A good read.<br /><br />Reviewed by Christine M. 0312641893|5.0|I read this book because of a recommendation from my school librarian, and found it wonderful!<br /><br />Regardless of whether this book appears on lists about \\"If you like the Hunger Games, you'll like...\\", it really isn't a dystopian.  There is a monarchy system of government in this book, not a dictatorship, and it isn't totalitarian, at least from what I could tell.  In fact, the prince, Kai, is Cinder's love interest.  This book is futuristic, maybe you would consider it post-apocolyptic because the society has been rebuilt after a World War IV, but not dystopian.<br /><br />That being said, I really liked the world building.  Not all the vocabulary is the same as ours, and the author allows the reader to figure out what things mean through inference.  For example, a portscreen seems to be our equivalent of a smart-phone or maybe tablet.  The text never says that it is a phone, but you could infer that.  I really enjoyed this approach to the world building.  Perhaps some people would find it annoying or confusing, but I feel it made things more realistic.  In addition, I could visualize the palace, the market, Cinder's work space, and so on very clearly.<br /><br />I liked Cinder, as well as the minor characters of Iko, Prince Kai, etc.  Both Iko, and Cinder as well, would use sarcasm from time to time, which often added humor to the story.  Prince Kai was a nice person, but had his share of self doubt/ flaws, and I definitely liked his character.  Even though the characters were well done, I hope to see even more development in the later books in the series.  Lavanna wasn't the strongest villain, and she was all bad and didn't have layers.<br /><br />That takes me to the fact the series is more than three books! Yay!<br /><br />The pacing was very wonderful, and I was able to get lost in the story.  In fact, I had an competition coming up the next day that I was worrying about, and Cinder was able to get me so absorbed that I stopped my fretting while I was reading it.  It is a fairly long book, but I was able to get through it quickly.<br /><br />The plot was a bit predictable, but it is a re-telling of Cinderella, so things are bound to be sort of predictable.  However, it still had many major changes from the story of Cinderella, so there is still some surprise.<br /><br />If you enjoy fairy-tale retellings, you will most likely find this book good, as well as science fiction fans.  I have read Scarlet, and am looking forward to Cress and Winter!

But the most important thing he did was provide meaning and understanding into how it all works there, and why some people are so drawn to the country.<br /><br />This was not an autobiography, but clearly is based upon several of the author's amazing life events. 0312427573|5.0|I just read a fantastic book ([[ASIN:0982253532 Hawaii: A Novel]]) where the author does an amazing job of getting into the heads of his ambitious and flawed human characters in a sweeping tale about greed in the Aloha State--a development deal where a Chinese billionaire wants to build a casino down the road from the Mormon church.  The author says in his acknowledgements that he was basically trying to write a \\"Tom Wolfe\\" book about Hawaii, and the book was even called \\"our Bonfire of the Vanities\\" in the Honolulu Weekly, so I went back and re-read Wolfe's book to see what the author was talking about, and thank god I did!  I used to think that your point-of-view characters had to be sympathetic people you could root for, but Wolfe provides this amazing narrative stance that gets you inside the heads of his characters, but somehow hovers above them at the same time inviting us to ridicule their selfish thoughts--but ridicule them in a way where we often have to admit that we would have been thinking the same thoughts in a similar situation.  Kramer's bumbling attempts to have an affair with The Girl with the Brown Lipstick, or Sherman's attempts to justify his affair with Maria (\\"I am, after all, a Master of the Universe!  Even my WIFE would understand!\\"), or the way Wolfe arcs Peter Fallow's connection to the journalistic scoop of the century (\\"Reverend Bacon provided me with all kinds of access--I owe my success to him!\\" becomes \\"I'm a genius--of course I did this all on my own!\\")--it's incredible how just as we laugh at these idiots, Wolfe gets us to look in the mirror at the same time and admit that even though it probably wasn't as extreme as cheating on your spouse, we all spend a lot of energy making \\"justifications\\" like this of one kind or another.<br /><br />None of this speaks to the story, which is just as thought-out as Wolfe explains it in his brilliant essay, \\"Stalking the Billion-Footed Beast\\" (Yes, I went and googled that too)--a how-to that the author of \\"Hawaii\\" certainly read, too.  Basically Wolfe says, here's how you write a Dickensian book for today: research it like it's a nonfiction journalistic endeavor, come up with characters that represent your locale (Dickens's London, contemporary Hawaii, Wolfe's New York City) from Low (crumbling South Bronx--at least through the eyes of white boys like Kramer and Sherman) to High (Park Avenue duplexes, Wall Street, swank restaurants, etc.), and set something in motion with a good What If that allows you to travel through all of it in great realistic convincing FUNNY detail INSIDE THE HEADS OF THESE CHARACTERS (\\"What if the Park Ave trust fundie winds up MAYBE running over the MAYBE thug who was MAYBE going to mug him, all in the climate of race-conflict-charged 1980s New York City?\\").  I've read that some of Wolfe's later works were \\"uneven,\\" but this one, all 600-plus pages of it, is as tight as a Hemingway short story.<br /><br />Finally, yeah it's a product of the 1980s, and the whole newspaper thing now seems ancient, but none of that matters.  I'm so glad I was reminded to pick this book up again, because it's one of those you'll read again and again like every five years or so.  LOVED IT!

B00BTHWFBK|5.0|There aren't many bands that get back together after several decades and succeed in recapturing in new songs the elements that made them successful in the first place - in fact, I can't think of any band that's done this (Led Zeppelin's 2007 reunion did, but with old songs). So, one of the first things Rick Rubin did when he got Black Sabbath together was to play them their first album, presumably in the hope that they could recapture the vibe that basically invented heavy metal. The good news for Sabbath fans is that they've come pretty close on what may be their last album. On a mix tape/playlist, the songs on 13 would sit pretty comfortably alongside the first four Sabbath albums - especially Black Sabbath and Paranoid. Songs on 13 are mostly medium-slow tempo and, with one exception, very heavy and unmistakably Black Sabbath.<br /><br />Rick Rubin should be congratulated for bringing out the best of these metal legends. Tony Iommi overcame his cancer treatment to prove he's still the riff-master, and I think his solos throughout the album are especially tasteful - no notes are wasted. Geezer Butler is still a monster on bass, as anyone who's seen him live recently would attest (Ozzy Osbourne gives him the highest compliment in the liner notes). Ozzy himself doesn't reproduce any of the high-pitched screams of the early days; rather he sticks to a lower register that would make it possible to reproduce these songs live during the current tour. Finally, while I'm sad to see Bill Ward didn't make it this far, Brad Wilk (Rage Against the Machine) fits in perfectly - 'surprisingly well' according to Geezer in a Guitar World interview - and he doesn't try to be too fancy or too heavy. Here's my subjective song-by-song breakdown of 13. I don't claim it's right or that everyone will agree with me, but it's how I feel about the songs after a handful of listens.<br /><br />End of the Beginning: I didn't warm to this song instantly, though it's the second `single' from thealbum and they've played it live already. The simple opening riff reminds me of the opening to their self-titled classic - like a tolling bell and the fast section of the song - from the 2:20 mark is awesome. 7/10<br /><br />God is Dead: this conveys a sense of menace from the get-go and I love the lyrics too. In fact I love everything about this song - Geezer's bass work near the end is fantastic. And it's not anti-God, so stay cool in the bible belt. 9/10<br />Loner: a bit more uptempo and more good lyrics. Teenage angst written by musos in their mid-60s - it shouldn't work. 8/10<br /><br />Zeitgeist: This track starts with some manic laughter from Ozzy (is there any other kind?) and quickly settles into a spaced-out vocal accompanied by faint guitar and soft drums ..... Yes, this is Planet Caravan part 2, complete with another trans-galactic space travel lyric. This is another classic for laying back with the lights out with a drink or smoke of choice. I'm glad they included this softer track, just like those early classic albums. 9/10<br /><br />Age of Reason: A killer track. Just a hint of an old song in one of the riffs (is it Hole in the Sky?) but just a hint - and it's a good thing. And the lyrics sum up all the troubles of the world in three verses - 'sustainable extinction, a fractured human race'. More teenage angst. Fantastic! 9/10<br /><br />Live Forever: This starts out with a great riff but I feel a bit let down by the chorus - whatever-forever, die-lie. At least it doesn't go on too long. 6/10<br /><br />Damaged Soul: Maybe this track succeeds best at time-travelling back to the late 60s. It's based on a simple, bluesey riff that reminds me a bit of latter Hendrix (the Blues album). Again, this is a good thing. 9/10<br />Dear Father: the closing song seems to be about the day of deliverance for child-molesting priests - a War Pigs for the church (Prayer Pigs?) 'Preacher of theocracy hiding your hypocricy under a false sanctity, holy phony empathy''. This song ends with the thunder, rain and tolling bells that announced the opening of the first song they wrote (Black Sabbath), suggesting that those bad priests are going down, down, down (to borrow from Tom Waits) rather than to the pearly gates. 9/10<br /><br />I recommend getting the bonus disc version if you can because those three tracks are well worthwhile. Methademic is (appropriately) the fastest song here, with Geezer's fingers and Brad Wilk's drumsticks working overtime - great lyrics and chorus too. Peace of Mind is just as good. Pariah isn't quite as good to my ears but that might be because I find the rhyming of pariah-messiah in the chorus irritating. As much as I like the first two songs, I can see why they kept them off the main album: their fast style is out of keeping with the album's medium-slow tempo (imagine sticking a 'Turn Up the Night' or 'Mob Rules' onto Master of Reality).<br /><br />So, overall this is a great, heavy Sabbath album that, surprisingly, stands up really well to their early work. Please note -I do not claim it is as good as those albums - rather that it's way way closer to them than we have any right to expect from a bunch of 65 year olds. And if it's their last they can be very proud of it. And fans can be grateful that they made it. I love it.

Speaking on several occasions, she left the impression that she would have preferred that her friends and neighbors had never read her books.  And her reaction to the HBO series seems to be one of shock and dismay that her neighbors have cable tempered with vast amounts of money.  So, in a weak and ill-conceived attempt to distance herself from her own creation, she strips poor Sookie of every bit of magic in her life, leaving her worse off than when she started out.  I feel sorry for Sookie.  I can not express a similar sentiment for her author.  Authors have literary license; but only as long as the publishers and reading public are willing to renew that license from time to time.  As for Dead Ever After, it might best be renamed Dead On Delivery.

B00BD2DRHW|5.0|I'm not always easy in reviews - a lot of people will give five stars just because they liked something, but I reserve five for games that really addict and challenge me.<br /><br />NOTE: I played this on my 7inch tablet; I would not want to play this on my phone, although I'm sure it's possible.  Then again, since I've gotten my tablet, I only use my phone as a phone, I can't imagine wanting to play games on it.<br /><br />So the game has several islands, and each island has a road that requires several bridges; each bridge is a \\"level.\\"  The graphics are of good quality (keep an eye out for the snowman), and the scene is shown with a grid on which you can place building elements.  Building elements need to start either at one of the anchor positions given, or continue from the ends of other building elements.  To start off with, you only have wood as building material; as you progress, you get to start using steel, concrete pillars, and suspension wires.  Each one has it's own cost structure, a base cost for one unit length plus more for each increase in length.  Wood and steal have limited lengths, the pillars and wires seem to be unlimited.  For example, you selected wood, then draw from the left anchor of the bridge across to the right; the wood will only extend to it's maximum length, then you lift your finger and the piece stays in place.  Touch the right anchor on that piece of wood, stretch it again to the right, then lift your finger to set it's position.<br /><br />Once you build your bridge, you put it in \\"play\\" mode.  That starts the physics engine, which tells you by color how much stress each element is under; too much stress causes the element to break.  If you think your bridge is worthy, you can test it out by sending either a pair of cars or a pair of trucks over it.  Ultimately, you want it to be stable enough for the trucks, so the only minor issue I have with the game is that the car test is useless since the truck test covers both testing fortrucks and cars, anyway.  I suppose if you're younger, or just not good at it, you might want to start with cars and then up the challenge to trucks, but it seems pointless to me.<br /><br />There are five achievements on each level; safe for cars, safe for trucks (the latter covering both), and achievements for building with restricted money, restricted resources, and restricted money while having an upper \\"stress\\" level on your bridge.  For example, you might need to build a bridge with under a certain amount of money where the highest stress on your bridge doesn't go over 50%.  There's a meter that shows the current maximum stress, and the coloring of the elements as they go under stress (going from green to red) shows you where the critical points are.  Sometimes the \\"restricted material\\" achievement includes other restrictions, like maximum number of pieces or maximum cost, but not always.<br /><br />You do NOT have to achieve all of these at once - you can build the bridge initially to get the first couple of badges, then make it cheap (and unsafe) to get the restricted money award, then beef it up to get the restricted stress award.  As I write this, the most helpful \\"critical\\" post says the money restrictions on some levels are \\"ridiculous,\\" they are NOT - they can be very challenging.  I did initially skip some, and finish them after completing the rest of the levels, and found that, with the extra experience, I was able to do them all fairly easily.  I did get every single achievement - it's challenging, but not ridiculously so.  Since you need only succeed in getting vehicles across to move on, you don't need to get every award to complete the map.<br /><br />I got this game for free, but having played it, I think it's easily worth the $2.00.  If there was another with different levels, I'd buy it - I was thinking about the original Bridge Constructor, but it seems like this is either a re-release of the same game, or nearly identical.  I want to make sure I have different levels.  If someone could tell me, that'd be great.<br /><br />So, point by point - the graphics were very well done, in my opinion.  I cannot rate the sound, as I almost always had it turned off.  The interface is limited by the touch screen; fat fingers mean misplaced elements all the time, but there's an \\"undo\\" button, and double tapping an element will remove it as well.  You will need to zoom in (pinch to zoom/unzoom works just fine) to make some of your trickier connections, but that's a feature, not a drawback (again, IMO).<br /><br />The game is challenging and fun, and watching your bridge disasters is as much fun as creating a functioning bridge; the way the vehicles crash is often comical and makes me chuckle... your success is determined by whether or not the vehicles make it across, and we've actually had a bridge that collapsed right at the end, but the vehicles made it across and we \\"won.\\"  It's hilarious (and very uncommon).  Both my son (14) and I greatly enjoyed this game, so I have to give it props.

B00140PK6O|5.0|If you are a fan of British historical dramas, then you could enjoy \\"Cranford.\\"  It may be a bit slower for some who might expect it to be something like \\"Downton Abbey,\\" but for those who enter into the backwater self-enclosed (or so it may seem at first) world of Cranford, you might well be charmed by it.<br /><br />Set in a small town in the 1840's England, the story of Cranford is based on the interactions of a finely-knit ensemble of characters--including the marvelous Judi Dench and other actors you will easily recognize if you have watched much British television or even the Harry Potter movies--as they either fight to hold on to the past or to bring in the future.  The stories range from poor squatters who must poach to bring food to the table to the lonely Lady of the Manor who seems cold at first, until you realize she is a woman in pain.  The town gossip with a feather in her cap is absolutely hilarious and you will never forget the incident with the cat and the buttermilk.<br /><br />Many of the main characters are women, including spinsters and widows, and they gossip about any and everything and are shocked (oh my!) by the idea of new muslins from Paris or who might have given new gloves to someone else and consult their book about what a bunch of flowers might mean when received.  Let alone all of the trouble that a single Valentine's Day card can cause.<br /><br />This is the story of a gentle age, when men and women still bowed to each other and people of a certain class all strived to adhere to an understood moral compass.  Yet, the story also addresses the trials of being poor and hungry at that time, of lost loves and opportunities, the misfortunes and grief of death, and how that same compass might be wrong when it comes to who deserved an education or to open a business of their very own.<br /><br />The storyline was based off of several novellas, with some additions, but it retains the witty commentary of the times by the author.  If you openyour heart to Cranford, it won't disappoint.

B00CISC2DA|5.0|As a 15 year BoC follower, I wanted to believe in all the rumors of a new album, but after numerous YEARS of silence it almost seemed like any hope left of new tunes might be fading. Maybe they really are done. Or if not, maybe I should try and stop caring so much because all the rumors that get debunked seem to crush my spirit in strange, sad ways. So that was it- I wasn't going to care so much about a new album. Wait, what, someone found a record in NYC on Record Store Day from Boards of Canada? And what, there's some kind of number code/clue on it and there's more spread across the world? What? Well, not caring went out the window and turned into a sort of mad obsession. No shame here, it felt good to finally get giddy like a kid at Christmas during the brilliant week long, multi-media code reveal campaign leading up to this release. And then the Tokyo midnight digital billboard audio and video teaser- the sound of BoC synths creeping out over the city. And then watching the beginning of the listening party out at the rundown waterpark in the middle of the desert. Anyway, yeah, after all that, it's safe to that never has an album release moved me this way, or gotten me more excited. As if 7 years of waiting for a new release didn't create enough buzz, now we have all this brilliant madness leading up to it. Oh boy.<br /><br />So, OK then- after all that- is it any good? With expectations pushed to an all-time high, will it meet them? Was it worth the wait?<br /><br />Well I guess you'll have to decide. For me the answer is yes.<br /><br />While I have never written a BoC review, because finding the right words was exhausting, and always felt incomplete when trying, I couldn't help but share my thoughts on Tomorrow's Harvest- though it can be tough to fully describe the love, insane attention to detail, the musical and instrumentation techniques used to create the duo's signature sounds, the immense feelings the music stirs up, the subtleties and hidden gems wovendeep within songs (reversed melodies and samples that reveal themselves after being played over and over or backwards), the mystique created by limited interaction with the public (and the idea of a bunker-style life tucked away in the countryside somewhere) and the cryptic symbols and numbers, etc, etc.<br /><br />So with all that mind- this album is interesting in multiple ways. The sound is a mix of classic BoC and some new elements/directions- most notably the arpeggiated synths used throughout (and more of a focus on chords/pads). The album also has a very cinematic feel- which ties in with the album title/art and the concept portrayed of a desolate, destroyed and abandon land (reaping what we sow perhaps, or as Mike put it in a recent interview: \\"It's not post-apocalyptic so much as it is about an inevitable stage that lies in front of us.\\"). There's a lot of open space and dissonance throughout, adding to this feel (Mike said one of the influences of the album was \\"grim 70s and 80s movie soundtracks\\"). So yeah- the album is darker (see Cold Earth- probably one of the bigger crowd pleasers), yet there are certainly some rays of sunshine throughout- refer to the great Nothing is Real (perhaps a sibling of Roygbiv), Palace Posy (that ending!) and New Seeds (may catch a few off guard). That said, if you are looking for a comparison, the album falls closer to Geogaddi and a little MHTRTC than anything else in their official catalog (with nods to the past old tunes as well). However, this album is very distinctly all its own (I'll point to New Seeds again and the gorgeously broken Jacquard Causeway as just a few examples). Production wise it's top-notch. Hi-fi here. The beats are heavy and strong, almost angry at times. The kicks are big and the snares are tight. There's an intense air hanging over this one to say the least. And though it's very easy to say some of the tracks should go on longer, it only adds to the definitive moments created here (per the recentinterview, they said this was done on purpose and was based around the idea of how soundtracks/scores are the same way- music seems to be plucked from a longer body of work and you never get to hear the full piece). Melodies say what they have to say and then they're gone. It's definitely a \\"play it again\\" album, for me anyway.<br /><br />Although I can see where this album could draw criticism (not everyone will like it), it calls for multiple plays, which has been no different than any of their catalog (I've heard a lot people say they were down on Geogaddi and Campfire Headphase at first and have since praised them as their favorite at one time or another). For those baptized BoC, this demands attention and will continue to reveal itself after many, many listens (a grower, as we would expect). And for the easter egg hunters- the recent interview has a quote from Mike saying that the album has more use of subliminals than any other previous album...<br /><br />Yep, they're back.

159562015X|3.0|StrengthsFinder 2.0 is not so much a book as it is an online personality profile. The major premise of this book is that people can take one of two avenues for success (in business, love, life, etc.), they can either focus their efforts on minimizing their weaknesses or focus their efforts on maximizing their natural strengths. StrengthsFinder 2.0 (as one might assume from the title), advocates the latter approach and is intended to assist the reader in identifying and maximizing his strengths.<br /><br />The book is divided into two parts. The first part is a presentation of the above mentioned thesis. In books if this genre, one would expect a dizzying amount of anecdotal evidence; but while a couple examples are given, Rath refreshingly bucks the trend and gives the reader \\"the meat.\\" The second part of the book is a brief description of various \\"strengths\\" (what you naturally do better than most of the population, alphabetically arranged) and tips for utilizing these strengths in the workplace.<br /><br />I'll admit that this is the only book I've reviewed that I haven't read cover to cover. Instead, I read the introductory section and the brief descriptions of my particular \\"strengths\\" (as determined by the on-line test). Since (for example), I don't have the \\"woo\\" strength, I don't feel competent evaluating it. However, I found that the strengths I did score well in accurately identified where I tend to excel and described my approach to things well. I feel that I've grown by understanding myself better and the strength-oriented advice has thus far been fruitful.<br /><br />The opening section on the premise was short and to the point. They forcefully argue for the reader to identify and focus on his strengths yet are careful to recognize that there may exist some weaknesses that necessitate minimizing. Their arguments are logical and compelling; I enjoyed reading it and buy into their thoughts.<br /><br />As other reviewers have pointed out, StrenghsFinder 2.0 is not so much as book as it is an online test cleverly marketed as a book. Aside from the first section (framing the premise), the rest of the book is pretty useless. After using the sealed code inside the book to access the online test, you receive a printer-friendly recapitulation of the \\"strengths\\" in section 2 that pertain to you. You may only take the test once and must purchase another book to gain another access code should you want a co-worker, spouse, or other person to take it. Like other reviewers, I had difficulty actually getting to the online test. I imagine this is party due to my lack of computer skills, and partly due to the awkward layout of their advertised web page. The test takes about 30 minutes, questions are timed (so that you don't think about it too much and answer the way in which your mind naturally works).<br /><br />In all, StrengthsFinder 2.0 is an excellent premise, a fine analysis of common strengths, and sound advice for persons exhibiting said strengths. But is it really a book? If book is defined as a series of sheets of paper with words/pictures on each page...yes, I suppose it is. If book is defined as a logically presented, complete thesis...then it's not really a book...it's more along the lines of a thick instruction manual, telling you how to take an on-line test. And while the premise, analysis, and advice is good, I argue that it's still incomplete. I find myself wanting to know more about my particular strengths--theory as well as practice, anecdotal as well as statistical. It is my hope that a follow up book (without an on-line component) is forthcoming.<br /><br />In all, I would recommend this \\"book\\" and believe it will be a great aid in leading to vocational excellence and fulfillment.

0375703861|3.0|Is this book a modern classic?  Sometimes the critics and I don't see eye to eye.  The ending to this book almost justified the 500 pages I had to read to get there, but not quite.  Archie Jones routinely makes life-and-death decisions by flipping a coin.  As bad decisions go, though, his are equaled by those of his long-time friend Samad Iqbal.  Samad longs to send his twin sons to Bangladesh so that they can become good Muslims and escape decadent Western influences.  Alas, he can afford to send only one and makes the ill-advised decision to send the studious son Magid, rather than the wayward son Millat.  Naturally, Magid embraces science there, eschewing religion, while Millat joins a fundamentalist Islam group here in the good old U.S.A.  The linchpin, though, is the Chalfen family, who host Millat and Archie's daughter Irie, along with their own son Joshua, in a school-imposed detention that reshapes everyone's lives.  Marcus Chalpen is a genetic researcher whose FutureMouse will prove to the world that genetic engineering can overcome the apparent randomness of fatal diseases.  I don't want to give too much away, but the finale brings together a volatile amalgamation:  Millat's jihad, Archie's mother-in-law and her band of Jehovah's Witnesses, the scientific community, and Joshua's animal rights group.  We can expect sparks to fly, but the surprise lies elsewhere.  The author treats the fragility of life in an interesting way, I must admit.  It literally turns on a dime, and a life saved can make a huge, unforeseen impact.  That impact may be positive or it may be negative or it may just stir the pot--or the plot, in this case.

Here are some tips to help judge between telling a Sony to a fake:<br /><br />A real Sony feels heavier in your hand and sits better. Also, the PS button is centered and the LED indicators are flush with the controller, and it has a formed tag in the back that is plastic, not a sticker.<br /><br />A FAKE is lighter, the PS button can be off center and the marking on the buttons can rub off after little use. If you have a fake, it will usually have just a sticker on the back that is poorly placed and the LED's are usually raised a bit. Also the battery is usually very poor, being dead within an hour of use. Finally, a fake will have a harder time pairing with the PS3.<br /><br />When you look to purchase an authentic Sony, make sure that you do your research first. Make sure that the company is very good and has plenty of 5 star ratings. Also, check their return policy and make sure that if it is a fake, you can return it with no hassle.<br /><br />If you are in doubt, buy it from a local store such as Best Buy where you shouldn't have a problem.<br /><br />Lastly, just keep in mind that some of the fakes are very good fakes and you might not be able to tell the difference until you really start to play with it.<br /><br />Good Luck!

B001PPLJIQ|5.0|The Reader is a provocative and heartbreaking film which tells the tale of a May-September affair between Hanna (Kate Winslet) and 15 year-old Michael (David Kross) in the decade after WWII. Beyond that, however, I believe the film is an allegory of the relationship between those Germans who were responsible for the Holocaust and those loved ones who came after them, and were forced to confront the horrible truths at a later time. It deals with the guilt, the anger, and the conflicting emotions therein. The story is heartbreaking because it reminds us that we are all struggling with guilt, emotions, and the legacy we have created for ourselves by our actions or inactions. It neither asks the viewer to forgive nor condemn. It merely shows us how similar we are to these people by putting their humanity in our faces.<br /><br />The movie is provocative for both the many nude/sex scenes between Michael and Hanna, and because it purposefully makes it a bit harder to blame or label, not only those responsible for the Holocaust, but all of the people in our history who have a connection between an evil deed and tacit complicity, regardless of the severity.  There is a powerful moment during the trial when a confused Hanna is questioned about her responsibilities as a guard and she innocently asks one of the judges, \\"What would you do?\\" None of the judges has an answer. Throughout all of history's atrocities, there are always those who were simply \\"doing their job.\\" It's so easy to imagine being the heroic person who would stand up a make a difference, but the fact is, few possess that much courage and conviction.<br /><br />Many recoil at the sexual perversity, but I think the intention was to make the audience uncomfortable. Not only did Hanna's seduction help create a person who would end up sharing her emotional void, but so too did the generation responsible for the Nazi atrocities force their shame onto the generation that followed them. The beauty of The Reader for me was in how these characters can speak for generations; the passion, the heartbreak, the guilt, the shame, the anger, and the eventual cold-blooded detachment.<br /><br />The acting is stellar throughout, with special props going to Kate Winslet and David Kross for their fearless portrayals and for breathing such believability into their characters. To engender empathy for people who run the gamut from despicable to emotionally empty is a nod to their acting skill. To compel me to watch the drama of such unsavory individuals is a testament to the power of this movie. The Reader might disturb you, anger you, disgust you, or even confuse you. Certainly one of the principal responsibilities for art is to provoke such reactions.<br /><br />P.S. I don't usually include info about the dvd extras, but since I watched them this afternoon, I thought I'd add a sentence or five. There are about a handful or so, focusing on the set design with a German woman who had left her native country during the post WWII period to get away from the drama, a lengthy bit with Kate Winslet getting into her older make-up, an interview with Kross and the director, and a bit with the music director. There are also quite a few deleted scenes that must've made it pretty far as they seem like a finished product, if you know what I mean. They are illuminating and at least one in particular should've been included as it not only bridges two scenes that have little continuity, but also features a very poignant conversation between Michael and an older truck driver. It's kind of chilling and should have been in the movie.e was in how these characters can speak for generations; the passion, the heartbreak, the guilt, the shame, the anger, and the eventual cold-blooded detachment.<br /><br />The acting is stellar throughout, with special props going to Kate Winslet and David Kross for their fearless portrayals and for breathing such believability into their characters. B00CISC2DA|2.0|So, a little disclaimer here first: I'm no professional at writing and I'm an amateur when it comes to critiquing. But this time I felt compelled to write something about the latest release from the eco-friendly brothers of IDM, something said from the trenches of one of those who pay for their music and aren't influenced by advertising dollars, or blinded by idol worship. I mean, the way some fans have been anticipating this album, you'd think BoC were God Himself, resurrected and ready to rescue the world from itself on release date. Such was the fervor for any new, official material from the brothers that after they uploaded their new single onto YouTube, their website crashed the same day due to traffic overload.<br /><br />I've listened to the whole album several times since Sunday and I gotta say, for keeping a rather cool head since the announcement for this album's release, I'm not moved much by it. If Mike and Marc were aiming here to make a dystopian horror soundtrack, then mission complete. You can practically do the zombie shuffle through it all (and even creepy zombie moans would sound appropriate in \\"Uritual\\"), as if hearing their Day of the Dead take on the aptly titled \\"White Cyclosa\\" wasn't evidence enough by then. It's almost like the brothers are pulling a BoC on their *own* past; everything that was once alive is now made ghostly, distant, and nostalgic.<br /><br />A third of the tracks are closer to being music (\\"Reach For The Dead\\"), some are mere curiosities (\\"Palace Posy\\"), and a few are tiresome (\\"Jacquard Causeway,\\" \\"New Seeds\\"). A real disappointment is that the breather tracks simply aren't great leaders (\\"Collapse,\\" \\"Transmisiones Ferox\\"), which is shocking when one considers that some fans were looking forward to those the most exactly because they typically lead into their best songs, e.g. \\"Ready Lets Go\\" leading into \\"Music is Math,\\" \\"Bocuma\\"'s lead-in to \\"Roygbiv,\\" \\"A Moment of Clarity\\" to \\"84 Pontiac Dream,\\" the opener for \\"Everything You Do Is a Balloon\\", etc. Without a doubt, the biggest lost opportunity of the album is \\"Split Your Infinities,\\" which opens with such a gorgeous 90 seconds that is so organic and other-worldly you feel buoyant, and then it fizzles out. This could've been the best track of the album, yet the melody fades out nearly as soon as it begins, repetitiously drumming onward for the final three minutes while some garbled & distorted voice makes some noises. What a waste!<br /><br />The one thing I've always enjoyed about BoC is that their music can sometimes catch on a moment of beauty and sustain it; a respite from the trouble of everyday life and of the world at large. \\"Cold Earth\\" and especially \\"Sick Times\\" are two good examples here of that signature sound of theirs that is simultaneously melancholic and yet uplifting. The finest example of this is on \\"Nothing Is Real,\\" a wonderful piece of chin-up after all the depressive atmospheres one must slog through to get to it. It is such a bright contrast to the surrounding darkness that it helps expose the weaknesses of the entire record; definitely the most enjoyable melody in the entire set (among whatever melody can be found to begin with).<br /><br />At the risk of sounding politically correct, I really do hope that Michael and Marcus find something positive and hopeful for their futures, and that they realize that not everything is as dead as this album impresses so hard. What does it say that fans expect more along the lines of Music Has The Right To Children and not of The Campfire Headphase? Rigid to change? Immune to hope? \\"I'd rather sadly roam the earth, long-face pointed downward, shuffling towards the inevitable end of our precious ecosystem\\"? [Shrugs] Who knows. Take my opinion for what it's worth, or don't. But I *do* know one thing: this deathly album's sound is BoC's eco-dirge, their requiem mass for the earth (contrast this to their previous LP, the most cheerful and enjoyable album of their career that\\"true\\" fans loathe so much). Having said that, I won't be extending my arm out to reach for the dead... I'd sooner keep it in the Campfire.<br /><br />UPDATE - April 27th, 2014: Having spent as much time laboring over this album as I've had, I'm adding this: I've only kept an EP's worth of tracks and that's being generous. The somber sonics of \\"Cold Earth\\" has Geogaddi written all over it, \\"Sick Times\\" is the ethereal place you'll end up in after passing under the Coke sign, and \\"Reach For The Dead\\" sums up the whole intended eco-collapse theme of the record, which fares fine on its own, sure... but \\"Nothing Is Real\\" (a funny title in hindsight, considering it's the most *tangible* song of the record) never ceases to make me ache and conclude that pursuing said theme for an entire LP's length was bad judgment on their part. And that's all for me, folks.

B0071XO8RA|5.0|Hugh Howey does not just create a story or a world in his Wool saga, he creates a vision of an entire society.  (More than one, actually, but you will have to read it all to see what I mean by that.)  Set at some point in the future, his characters live in a world where the ultimate sin is to ever want to leave.  How and why things got that way is the crux of the story, so I will skip the spoilers.<br /><br />Mr. Howey's world is rich, complex, and engaging, but his stories read at warp speed.  There is enough detail to fully build his world without leaving the reader mired in the minutiae that so often seems to bog stories like these down.<br /><br />Mr. Howey has decided to go the route of self-publishing, and while in general this has made me cringe a bit on Amazon, in this case, it hasn't been a mistake.  His books have been well edited, are tightly plotted, and are an overall good read.  There are a couple (very few) small typographic errors to be found, but unlike many of the independent works, they do not detract from the story, and are almost unnoticeable as the reader absorbs his prose.<br /><br />I truly enjoyed the Wool Omnibus, and all of the works which followed.  In my humble opinion, Mr Howey has written a classic on par with Soylent Green or the works of George Orwell.  I suspect the greater publishing world is beginning to notice that, as I have heard Wool mentioned on NPR and outside of the Kindle/Amazon community.<br /><br />Strongly recommend this book.

0156027321|5.0|I'm sure you have read many different stories about castaways and getting lost at sea. Titanic, Gilligan's Island, Castaway, Swiss Family Robinson, and the TV show Lost. All of these and many others are unique in their own way. However, have you ever heard of one that involves a young boy alone? And if you have, have you heard of a story about a young boy floating at sea, with a live Bengal Tiger?<br /><br />The novel, Life of Pi, by Yann Martel, tells such a tale. The main character is Pi Patel, and he is the protagonist because he is the only relatable character. He is also the narrator; he saves himself and the tiger, and learns a lot about himself and life during the course of the novel. Another character is Richard Parker, (the Bengal tiger), and he is important to the story because he is the only other character on the boat with Pi and keeps Pi alive.<br /><br />The conflict that these characters are facing is that Pi is lost at sea, for 227 days, in a lifeboat, with a 450-pound Bengal tiger and depleting food and water with no end in sight.<br />This story begins in India. Pi has a mother, a father, a brother, family friends, and many people he looks up to. He finishes school early, and looks for new excitement. As a 16-year-old, he adopts three religions; Hinduism, Christianity, and Muslimism. This gives him a different perspective on life, and even though his mother and father don't support this decision, they do not protest. His father owns a zoo. The Patel family decides to move from india to Canada, by ship with some of their zoo animals, but unfortunately in the middle of the journey their boat sinks. Pi ends up on a lifeboat, with a hyena, an orangutan, a wounded zebra, and Richard Parker. The hyena eats the zebra and the orangutan and then is killed by Richard Parker. Pi feels like he will not survive this journey with an active tiger, but then finds supplies in the boat and Richard Parker lets it be known that he will not be of harm to Pi. Pi fishesand gets adequate food and water for him and his companion, until they go blind from lack of nourishment. In that same time frame, they hear another castaway on a lifeboat, who is also blind, and Pi feels like he could have some hope until Richard Parker dismantles Pi's new friend. Other close encounters ensue, such as a ship that passes by but does not see their small boat and landing on a dangerous island that doesn't fit well to their human and animal needs. After 227 days, Pi and Richard Parker land in Mexico. This is where our friend's story ends.<br /><br />The ending of the book wasn't satisfying for me. Their conflict was resolved but not in the best way, because in my opinion, after all Pi went through, the interrogators he shares his story with should respect him more. He went through a lot, and by them not believing his story, it shows that everything he went though was all for nothing.<br /><br />There could be many different themes for this book. The first is that there is always more self-discovery to do. Before his life changing journey, Pi has three religions. He has learned a lot about himself and the quantities of life. During his experience, he must be alone. The only things he can rely on are his religions and his depleting sense of hope. Without these two, he would never have survived. He would have given up, stopped feeding himself, and not looked forward towards the possibilities that could arise. After his adventure, he discusses how he feels different. He has learned a lot about himself and the world and the harshness of things, which have changed him into a different person than the one we meet at the beginning.<br /><br />Overall, I liked this book because it wasn't boring. For example, it never had a dry moment, even when they were just floating around the ocean. The author only included parts of the story that were interesting to read.<br /><br />The book was predictable because we knew from the beginning that Pi would get rescued, as the book is told from Pi's point of view to a storyteller, and we occasionally get glimpses of that outside scene. The ending of the book was fulfilling due to the fact that you felt happy for them when they were finally out of their situation. The author designed this book so that you had a real bond with the characters, and you were rooting for their conflict to be resolved the entire time. My favorite parts were when Pi and Richard Parker bonded.<br /><br />I would recommend this book to others because it provided a new perspective on life. I truly feel that it changed my point of view, and so because of this I would rate it a 9.3 out of 10. Do not read this book if you get bored or frustrated easily. The style of writing was very clear and made it easy to enjoy the book. The characters were not villains, yet we knew them very well and learned to relate with them. The plot was a fantastic idea, even if it wasn't a true story, and in conclusion this book would be worth anyone's time to read.

0449237567|5.0|I have been conducting a long march through science fiction novels [so much of what's in the movies is so bad], comparing and contrasting both contemporary and classic, as well as re-reading novels I read in my teens and 20s, over three decades ago, to see how they hold up.<br /><br />Recently I read [[ASIN:0812550706 Ender's Game (Ender, Book 1)]], specifically because it is being made into a movies, and I wanted the experience of the novel first;  I re-read [[ASIN:0743273567 The Great Gatsby]] for the same reason, and that was way better than when I had to read it in high school, and I enjoyed the movie as well on its own terms. They are fun on the action level, and maybe if I read more in each series they would be more thought provoking.<br /><br />And now I've finished Gods.  In contrast with the war novels, while there are aliens, nobody kills anybody.  This is a novel of ideas, and the conflict is between physicists: who gets credit for what, and who is looking at the long range consequences of scientific breakthroughs.  In this case, more lethal than even the thermonuclear bomb.<br /><br />Asimov, being the polymath that he was, and the author of hundreds of nonfiction books, imbues the story with real principals: the strong nuclear force, the weak nuclear force, the laws of thermodynamics and more. The prose is, well, prosaic, but it certainly doesn't detract from the story nor call attention to itself.  The characters are well defined, although the humans are closer to stereotypes. B00BTHWFBK|5.0|For me, it all started at a record store in 1970 where I heard 'Paranoid' blasting over the store speakers. At 13 years of age, it made quite the impression. That's what started \\"the obsession\\". Like sitting in English class not hearing a word the teacher said because I had just read an article about the forthcoming \\"Volume 4\\" in Circus magazine. So here we are in 2013, and after false starts, bad blood, and lawsuits galore, three-quarters of My Favorite Band Of All Time deliver a comeback album. I feared many things: 1) the loss of Bill Ward - well, he's not there, and it sucks, plain and simple. But I've dealt with many a band line-up change over the years, and it always comes down to one thing: HOW'S IT SOUND?? In this case, an admirable job by Brad Wilks. Enough of a 70's vibe throughout to remind you of Sabbath past, but a lot of muscle where needed to drive these surprisingly heavy and adventurous riffs and arrangements. Ozzy may be right: Ward may not have been able to handle this album properly in his current state of health. 2) Tony Iommi's cancer - would it negatively impact his playing and compositional abilities? What a relief to hear NO!! The Master is forceful, adept and monstrous on this album, churning out 70 minutes of classic Black Sabbath heavy riffs along with some burning solo work. 3) Rubin's production - whatever criticisms people may have here (I basically have none) he can be thanked for bringing the band back to their roots. Do I wish it was an album full of 'The Wizard' and 'Into the Void'? Yes. Do I wish the old Rodger Bain sound of \\"Master of Reality\\" was resurrected here? Hell, yeah! But no complaints for an album full of songs that could basically have found their way on to any album in the \\"Volume 4\\" - \\"Sabbath Bloody Sabbath\\" - \\"Sabotage\\" trifecta. And there are moments of earlier influence: 'Damaged Soul' is like Cream on steroids, and could easily have found its way on to Black Sabbath \\"1\\". And 'Zeigeist' is a nice reprieve from all the volume with it's nod to 'Planet Caravan' or 'Solitude'. 4) Ozzy's vocals - OK, we all know how he can sound in concert. Like many aging musicians, his voice is pretty spent, and now resides in a generally lower register. But here, he simply reminds me of why Tony's riffs and Ozzy's vocals have always been like Sean Connery and James Bond, or William Shatner and Star Trek: IT WORKS, plain and simple. Ozzy's voice here is surprisingly effective, lending many of the songs an almost spooky, gothic quality, reminiscent of the way he sang on the first two albums. 5) Anyone criticizing this Return Of The Great Ones need only listen to \\"Technical Ecstasy\\" and \\"Never Say Die\\" to remind themselves where these guys were thirty years ago and where they are now. 6) This, thankfully, sounds NOTHING like the Dio era! Oh, yes, this all could have turned out SO BAD, but it turned out SO GREAT! Thanks, guys, for making me feel 15 again!

B00CISC2DA|4.0|\\"Perhaps the only album in three years (since 2010's Gorillaz Plastic Beach) that I waited and anticipated for to buy at first release and on hard copy. Scottish electronic music duo bring back their experimental and moody flair with Tomorrow's Harvest, a collection of 17 pieces complementing much of their nostalgic, analogue-sounding cues with more high-definition sound and menace.<br /><br />The album takes much more a Geogaddi direction in its sound design and mood than Campfire Headphase (which is phenomenal in case the haters deny) and Music Has the Right... At first a hard one to try and grasp as the album acted a bit sluggish and compromising at the beginning, the second try at listening to it was a bit more rewarding which is why I did not automatically give it a 5-star review. Issues of immediacy and variety did arise in the beginning as I heard this album. The beginning with the recently known and very sci-fi-like Reach for the Dead did not kick-start with much surprise or intrigue as An Eagle in Your Mind or Chromakey Dreamcoat (the first ever BoC song I ever heard, in truth).<br /><br />As I got grasp of much of their signature elements and the funky-psychedelic rhythms, then I started developing my already obsessive interest of the album. Within respected order, Jacquard Causeway (while repetitive, it was still spectacular with much of its Burial-esque drumming pattern), Telepath (clearly BoC from the haunting voice sample and editing), Palace Posy (which sounds like slow-tuned jungle meets French electronica act Deep Forest), Nothing is Real (cheerful, beautifully done, and romantic in a kind of way), New Seeds (calculating and a bit like the Tortoise song, Djed), and the difficult-to-pronounce Semena Merlvykh (a foreboding, extremely well-done orchestral type of piece that could have fitted the Prometheus soundtrack any day and made it more appreciable to its already displeased fans). The artwork is also complementary with some abstract and naturalistic designs of Californian desert, military buildings, and grainy shots of outer San Francisco which are well-done in its 8mm like film but kind of contradictory to their image as either Canadians or Scottish.<br /><br />The unpredictability and nature vibe is what them interesting in the first place. I got this album for that reason just mentioned and because I am a rather big fan of their work. Thankfully, much of it did not disappoint and they stayed to their guts unlike the increasingly-pop Royksopp and Hot Chip, or the same old, same old Autechre. Thankfully, BoC reign high and strong with their strange melodic standards compared to other musicians who have had long waits from its respected fans like My Bloody Valentine, Daft Punk, and David Bowie.\\"ic designs of Californian desert, military buildings, and grainy shots of outer San Francisco which are well-done in its 8mm like film but kind of contradictory to their image as either Canadians or Scottish.<br /><br />The unpredictability and nature vibe is what them interesting in the first place.
